Home Builders Sweeten Incentives --- As Demand Slows, Developers Offer Free Heat, Golf Passes, Extra Help With Closing Costs
By Kemba J. Dunham and Ruth Simon
1256 words
10 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

AS THE HOUSING market's red-hot sales pace shifts to a slow burn, some home builders and developers are beginning to offer buyers a richer array of incentives.

Faced with rising inventories of unsold homes and reluctant buyers in many markets, a number of builders and developers are ratcheting up their promotional efforts. One developer is offering as much as $10,000 toward closing costs, while a home builder is throwing in golf-club memberships. Some incentives are available to any buyer, while others are tied to the buyer making use of a builder's preferred mortgage lender. Builders also are pitching higher commissions or special bonuses to real-estate agents.

Other deals are more creative: A Miami developer is offering to buy back its condo-hotel units at a premium after 18 months. Another builder, taking a cue from last summer's auto makers' deals, is offering "employee pricing" discounts in certain markets.

The moves come as Toll Brothers Inc., a luxury-home builder based in Horsham, Pa., this week lowered its forecast for how many houses it expects to sell in 2006. It cited, among other things, softening demand in a number of its markets, including Chicago, Las Vegas, and Washingon, D.C., and certain parts of Northern California, Maryland and Florida.

Builders often use various types of incentives to help sell homes, even during the strong market in recent years. This practice typically picks up late in the year as companies become eager to close sales and get properties off their books before the end of the year.

But some analysts say incentives have gotten more generous, as rising mortgage rates and elevated home prices have slowed sales. Traffic to new-home communities is down, waiting lists are disappearing, and inventories are inching up, analysts say. Ivy Zelman, a housing analyst at Credit Suisse First Boston, says builders' incentives typically work out to be 2% to 3% of the sales price. In some markets, incentives currently are running as high as 5%, she says.

When Brian Widic, a 34-year-old systems engineer, bought his new home in Leesburg, Va., a year ago from Winchester Homes, Mr. Widic says he felt lucky to receive about 1% of the home's value, or about $10,000, off the closing costs. Now Mr. Widic says he is being bombarded with mailings from various home builders offering a slew of incentives that range from a finished basement to Redskins season tickets. "I would certainly have a little bit more bargaining power today," he says.

A September survey of 488 single-family home builders by the National Association of Home Builders found that 58% were offering nonprice sales incentives, compared with 51% six months earlier.

For home buyers, you might have to ask if a builder is offering any special deals, as not all builders publicize their offerings. When it comes time to dicker, buyers are likely to have more success if they ask for upgrades rather than for a price cut, says Rhonda Duffy, broker-owner of Duffy Realty in Atlanta.

Offers of reduced closing costs or other incentives tied to using the builder's preferred lender should be examined closely and compared with what may be available from other mortgage lenders.

In Las Vegas, the number of builders offering incentives is increasing as the market slows, says Larry Murphy, president of SalesTraq, which tracks the Las Vegas housing market. Among the recent offerings: granite countertops, a $30,000 swimming pool and a one-year pass to a local public golf course valued at roughly $3,000. Denver-based MDC Holdings Inc. is offering $7,500 toward closing costs or $20,000 of upgrades on homes priced around $375,000 at one of its Las Vegas divisions.

Waverton Homes, a builder in Portsmouth, Va., recently increased the amount it will pay toward closing costs to $4,000 from $2,500 if buyers in its Estates at Creekside Landing development use the builder's preferred lender and close before year-end. The builder is also offering agents a $1,500 bonus. "There's no question that it has slowed down," says Penny Boyd, the selling agent for the projects, where homes are priced from $433,000 to $530,000.

Builders are also pitching incentives at real-estate agents. In Phoenix, Los Angeles builder KB Home is paying agents 4% commissions on the entire sales price on homes at certain area developments. KB also is offering home buyers between $1,000 and $2,000 off the closing costs on spec homes priced in the range of $300,000 to $400,000. The catch? Buyers have to finance their homes through Countrywide KB Home Loans, a joint venture between Countrywide Financial Corp. and KB Home.

In Miami Beach, The Mitchell Companies recently rolled out a special offer for buyers who purchase condo-hotel units in its Melia Royal Palm project before the sales office opens. The company is offering units at 10% below the list price and then promising to repurchase them for 12% above that price in 18 months. Buyers who want to keep the units must repay the 10% discount.

And Elad Group Florida, a unit of Elad Group Ltd., recently advertised "Sizzling Savings" of up to $10,000 toward closing costs to some buyers who purchase units in its new condominium development in Miramar, Fla.

Many builders are reluctant to admit that their current incentives are any different than their typical offerings at this time of year. Any appearance of a decline in home prices or profits can spook investors and send stock prices lower.

Centex Corp., a big Dallas-based builder, announced last month that it was rolling out an "employee pricing" promotion, in which buyers at one Atlanta development will be offered a 5% discount and up to $20,000 in additional incentives on select homes if they financed through the company's mortgage unit. Normally offered to Centex employees, the discounts are being offered to the general public for a limited time. Centex offers similar programs in St. Louis, Port St. Lucie and San Antonio. A Centex spokesman says the incentive program is a limited offer and is not a "big deal."

Pulte Homes Inc., based in Bloomfield Hills, Mich., recently offered a free gas fireplace, free hardwood floors on the main level, free upgraded cabinets and free washer/dryer at one of its Maryland developments. It also offered a choice of free heat for six months, a free 42-inch plasma television set or free window coverings in the amount of $5,000. "I know this is unbelievable, but it is true!" reads one emailed advertisement.

Sue Ko, a realtor in Arlington, Va., who received the email, says the Pulte offer "was one of the most incentive-heavy ones" she has ever seen. But Jim Zeumer, Pulte's vice president of investor and corporate communications, says it's all smoke and mirrors. "In terms of price, you're getting a plasma TV, but the rest of the `free' listings are all standard in the house," he adds.

                                Looking for Deals 
 
  Some builders are boosting incentives to speed sales. Buyers should keep 
in mind: 
 
  -- Not all home builders publicize special offers, so don't be afraid to 
     ask. 
 
  -- It's easier to negotiate for upgrades than for price cuts.

Personal Finance
Buying a Condo Is Getting Trickier --- Location Remains Crucial As Prices Continue to Soar; Avoid `White-Vanilla Boxes'
By Lauren Baier Kim
879 words
9 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Despite a slowdown in overall housing sales, the market for condominiums remains strong.

The median price for a condominium surpassed that of a single-family home for the first time last year, according to the National Association of Realtors, or NAR. And it appears that this year will be the 10th consecutive record-setting year in terms of rising condo prices and the number of condo sales, with median condo price tags still above those for single-family homes.

In September, the median condo price of $213,600 was up 9% from September 2004, according to the NAR, and condo sales activity was 10% higher.

Yet some observers question whether the boom will last. The supply of condo units has increased at a record pace, according to the National Association of Home Builders. Much of the demand has been supported by speculative buyers rather than occupants, said Chip Brown, senior vice president and co-director of production at CWCapital, a national multifamily and commercial real-estate lender in Needham, Mass.

In the face of a market that may be peaking, condo buyers should keep the following five tips in mind, especially when looking in a market where prices have risen sharply, such as Miami or San Diego.

Location is the key factor. Location is likely to be the biggest factor in your condo's resale value.

Buying in a popular neighborhood will help assure a property maintains its value, although sellers might not always reap such big gains. Buyers seeking large payoffs are likely to be better off purchasing in a new or emerging hot market, though that strategy is a bit riskier.

Buying on a waterfront is usually a good bet for retaining value. Even on the water, not all locations are created equal. End units are preferable, because they have more windows, as are units that face the beach (instead of the parking lot) and ones that have outdoor balconies.

Avoid "white-vanilla boxes." Distinguishing features help when it comes time to sell. Your condo should have at least one quality that will pique interest, agents say. This something special could be a bit of private outdoor space, a view, a garden or a celebrity resident, said Rob Gross, a senior vice president at Prudential Douglas Elliman in New York -- "whatever gives it a bit of cachet."

Condo buyers' wish lists include granite countertops and high-end kitchen appliances. Stand-alone refrigerator drawers are popular. These can be installed in the kitchen, or in the master bedroom for refreshments. Other in-demand features include hardwood and bamboo floors, large windows and high ceilings.

Popular amenities include a 24-hour doorman, a live-in superintendent, bike-storage rooms and an off-lobby refrigerator for grocery deliveries. Some buildings are installing wine rooms in their basements, while others offer pools and recreational centers.

Look for name recognition. A brand name may help fetch a premium price. Donald Trump's residential projects, such as Trump International Hotel &Tower hotel-condominium buildings in Chicago, New York and Las Vegas, tend to generate more buyer interest, Mr. Gross said, especially among foreign investors.

More high-end projects are being built by well-known designers or architects. French designer Philippe Starck is teaming up with developer Jorge Perez to create Icon Brickell, a condominium complex in Miami. Other well-known names who have gotten into the condo act include architects Richard Meier and Michael Grave.

If your builder isn't a household name -- and most aren't -- try to make sure that the builder is at least well-known, and reputable, within the local market.

Weigh old versus new. If you are buying in an older condo complex, make sure that the building and its grounds have been kept in good condition. If your building isn't kept up properly, your unit will likely be less attractive to buyers.

When buying a condo as a short-term investment and banking on quick price appreciation, the better bet is to buy a new condo, preferably in an initial offering, says Florence Shapiro, an associate with Prudential Americana Group in Las Vegas.

If you're willing to invest some elbow grease, purchasing a condo that needs work might be the way to go, says Nick Patterson, an agent with Coldwell Banker Residential Brokerage in Chicago, who deals with many first-time condo buyers.

Look for good management. A good condo association is crucial to maintaining your condo's value. The association will generally cover expenses to insure and maintain the property, so it is important that it is professionally managed and has the funds to make needed repairs. The type of fixes can vary widely, such as fresh interior paint in the common areas and a new roof, so a condo association's reserve funds are important.

Take a good look at the association's budget: It will help determine the services you will receive and the dues you will be charged.

The Property Report
Blueprint / Las Vegas
By Maura Webber Sadovi
838 words

Corrections &Amplifications

CONSTRUCTION on Qualcomm Inc.'s new facility in North Las Vegas is scheduled to begin by the end of this year. The Blueprint column in Wednesday's Property Report incorrectly said construction would begin in 2007.

(WSJ Nov. 14, 2005)

Big Growth Shapes

City Beyond `Strip'

Casinos and hotels in Las Vegas have about $16 billion in development projects planned or under way, many of them along the legendary Strip. While all of the construction will add 23,000 hotel rooms in the region by 2009, along with a small city's worth of shopping, entertainment and condominiums, a subtler though profound change is taking place in the desert beyond the neon lights.

The draw of plentiful jobs in the casino industry, combined with housing costs that remain slightly more affordable than neighboring California, makes the Las Vegas region the country's fastest-growing major metropolitan area. Its population rose 83% from 1990 to 2000, according to the U.S. Census Bureau, and the most recent estimate stands at 1.7 million, according to Economy.com.

The population boom and a fast-paced economy are transforming every sector of the region's real estate. Higher land prices and record levels of new-home construction are expanding the region's boundaries as developers scramble to accommodate the influx with new malls, offices and schools.

"The city is changing from a gaming community into a metropolis," said Richard Lee, vice president of First American Title Co., which provides real estate consulting to the local hotel industry. Though Mr. Lee said he believes casinos will always be a core part of the economy, "the new Las Vegas is not just about casinos."

The housing market has experienced some of the region's most astounding growth. The median home price almost doubled in three years to $300,100 in the second quarter from $159,800 in 2002, according to the National Association of Realtors. That's just below the median of $312,600 in the high-cost western U.S.

Some 120 mid- to high-rise condominium projects are planned in the region, while conversions of rental apartments into condos helped lower the apartment vacancy rate to 4.1% in the third quarter, the lowest in 54 major markets surveyed by Property &Portfolio Research Inc., a Boston-based real estate research firm.

With land prices rising at annual double-digit rates and 80% of the region's land under federal government ownership, home builders are focusing on higher-density projects and are pushing beyond the mountains that ring the valley into former farm towns such as Mesquite and Pahrump, says Monica Caruso, a spokeswoman for the Southern Nevada Home Builders Association.

Las Vegas Mayor Oscar Goodman, a former criminal-defense lawyer, also is working to revitalize the city's downtown, which he sees as a place where locals can live and work. He says he has gotten tougher on crime and encouraged the revitalization of the older buildings and casinos along Fremont Street, the city's main drag. "People euphemistically called it mature, but it was seedy," Mr. Goodman said.

He also says he hopes to expand downtown's boundaries with a project called Union Park that will include a performing-arts center, an Alzheimer's research clinic and a new city hall on 61 acres of former rail yard just west of the existing downtown. Mr. Goodman's ambitious vision has had some setbacks lately. Long-running negotiations with Related Cos., one of the country's biggest developers, broke off recently, and the city has decided to act as its own developer.

A booming job market, especially in tourism, construction and services, increased employment by 7.7% in the 12 months ended in June, the fastest growth rate of any major city, according to PPR. Office buildings have also benefited from the job growth, with vacancies declining almost three full percentage points to a below-average 13.6% in the third quarter from the year earlier. Developers are responding. Work is set to begin this year on a 265,000-square-foot downtown office tower near the proposed Union Park project, and Qualcomm Inc. plans to begin construction in 2007 on a facility on 32 acres in North Las Vegas.

Housing aid for the A-list: discounted condos --- Developers quietly reduce prices on luxury units to attract famous faces
By Deborah Schoeneman Special to The Wall Street Journal
1116 words
7 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
31
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

AT THE MERIDIAN, a condominium tower that opened last month on New York's Long Island, the asking price for two-bedroom units on a high floor with beach views is $1.8 million.

Unless you are Billy Crystal.

The actor, who grew up on Long Island, has been offered one of those two-bedrooms for $1.6 million. The offerer: the building's marketing agent, who thinks Mr. Crystal's name would help to sell units.

"You need to find innovative ways to sell apartments these days," says the agent, Michael Shvo, who has yet to hear back from the actor.

They have been on the freebie train for years, from gratis designer fashions to restaurant tabs that never materialize. Now, celebrities are on the receiving end of another shopping break the rest of us don't get: a real-estate discount. With an increasing number of luxury condominiums coming onto the market in New York, Las Vegas and Miami, developers in the U.S. are quietly offering price cuts or sweetened deals to famous faces. The practice is an increasingly important marketing tool to attract full-price buyers in the condominium market, a field some investors worry is becoming overbuilt and attracting a disproportionate number of speculators.

"The same way every designer wants to dress Nicole or Gwyneth for the Oscars, every developer wants to say someone fabulous bought in the building or even looked at it," says New York broker Michele Kleier of Gumley Haft Kleier Inc. "All of a sudden, the building becomes an `it' project."

There is nothing illegal about celebrities getting preferential treatment, of course. But because the apartments involved are so pricey, the deals often are conducted in secret. Unlike typical home buyers, celebrities often purchase apartments in corporate names to avoid having the details published. For the same reason, deals are sometimes structured so that the unit remains in the developer's name. Often, builders won't discuss discounts, fearing they will give the impression their projects aren't selling on their own.

Celebrities, too, are reluctant to talk about the perk because it could create an image issue. Mr. Crystal's publicist, for one, confirms the star was offered a discount on a high-floor, two-bedroom unit at the Meridan but declined to comment further.

How do the deals work? Typically, developers and brokers say a celebrity resident accepts a discount in exchange for allowing their name be used to promote the project. Often, that means having a publicist "leak" the A-list name to gossip columns, or trot it out in news releases and on billboards. Having a celebrity neighbor doesn't increase values necessarily, brokers say, but it does get more potential buyers looking at a building.

In the case of Cipriani Club Residences at 55 Wall St., luxury condos in New York City slated to open next fall, the celebrities who have allowed their names to be attached to the project are the model Naomi Campbell, actor Mickey Rourke and Margherita Missoni, daughter of the Italian knitwear family.

During an industry preview party of the building in September, co-developer Guiseppe Cipriani touted the building's amenities, including a wine cellar, 24-hour concierge service -- and the trio of famous faces as future neighbors. "They are my friends, and they are moving here," he said, as marketing agents handed out promotional brochures. In one photo, Mr. Cipriani is shown holding a cocktail shaker, standing next to Ms. Campbell, who is wearing a low-cut dress. In another, Mr. Rourke is shown smoking with friends; Ms. Missoni is pictured with a man's head cradled in her lap. Ads using some of the images have run in Vanity Fair and the New York Times Magazine.

All three received a discount in exchange for allowing the use of their photos in the promotions, says Mr. Cipriani, declining to be more specific. Ms. Campbell's publicist, Rob Shuter, says her modeling fee was deducted from the condo cost. He wouldn't say how much was deducted or how much she paid for her unit. Publicists for Mr. Rourke and Ms. Missoni didn't return phone calls seeking comment.

While modern-day condo developers have jumped on the celebrity discount as a sales tool, the practice goes back almost 100 years, or more. According to author Steven Gaines, in 1912, developer Douglas Elliman (who started the eponymous New York brokerage firm) gave Nobel Peace Prize winner Elihu Root about 50% off the rent to move into 998 Fifth Ave., one of the city's first fancy apartment buildings. More recently, Donald Trump says he gave Johnny Carson a discount on an apartment in Trump Tower in 1983 "because it was good for the building."

With increasing competition in the luxury-condo market, developers are looking sell their projects as quickly as possible. According to the National Association of Home Buyers, condos account for 35% of all new-home construction in the U.S. this year, up from 20% in 1998. At the same time, condo prices are rising quickly, with the median price reaching a record $221,700 in June, up 14% from a year earlier, according to the National Association of Realtors.

In some cases, it is the celebrity -- or his representatives -- who approach the developer. Izak Senbahar, who developed 165 Charles St. in New York, says actor Leonardo DiCaprio's broker, Susan-Gilder Hayes, asked to get the star a 20% discount last year on an approximately $5 million condo. According to Mr. Senbahar, the actor visited the building six times. "The message to me was, `You have to give him a 20% discount,' " says Mr. Senbahar. "I said, `I don't think so.' We don't need gimmicks." A spokesman for Mr. DiCaprio declined to comment. Mrs. Hayes declined to comment. Nine of the building's 31 units, which range in price from $5.3 million to $13 million, still are available after a year of being on the market.

Of course, many residents will have more luck seeing their famous neighbors on the big screen than in their buildings. New York real-estate broker Haley Binn recently bought a loft in Tribeca after reading in a gossip column that the actress Meryl Streep bought a penthouse in the building for $8.95 million. (Ms. Streep paid full price, says the developer. A spokesman for Ms. Streep didn't return phone calls.) Says Mrs. Binn: "I'm still waiting to run into her in the elevator."

The Economy
Productivity Rises At Fastest Pace In Over a Year
By Joi Preciphs
433 words
4 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

U.S. productivity grew at the fastest clip in more than a year during the third quarter, signaling steady growth for the economy, and a gauge of inflationary pressure dropped, a Labor Department report showed.

Meanwhile, business activity in the nonmanufacturing sector is recovering from the hurricanes that devastated the Gulf Coast, while the number of unemployment claims filed last week fell to prehurricane levels. Also, factory orders sank in September, and a measure of future housing demand slipped.

Nonfarm business productivity surged at a 4.1% seasonally adjusted annual rate from July through September, the Labor Department said yesterday. The increase, which exceeded economists' expectations, was about twice that of the second quarter's 2.1% rise. The robust growth helped drive down unit labor costs, which fell to a 0.5% annual rate after a 1.8% rise in the second quarter.

If productivity is rising smartly, employers can boost wages without fueling inflation. But many analysts don't predict a sharp rise in wages.

"The labor pool isn't tight enough to do that," said economist Kathleen Camilli, president of Camilli Economics, a New York City economic and financial-services firm. "It's happening selectively in some areas, but by no means is it widespread as it was in the 1990s."

Some indicators offer encouraging signs that consumers are still spending. The National Association of Realtors said its index for pending sales of existing homes slipped 0.3% in September to 128.8 -- the second-highest reading on record and 3.3% higher than in September 2004. Several major retailers are reporting that October sales appear solid based on preliminary numbers.

In addition, the Institute for Supply Management reported its Nonmanufacturing Business Activity index rebounded to a reading of 60 in October from September's depressed 53.3 reading, which was attributed to the hurricanes. Eleven of 17 industries reported growth during the month, including entertainment, insurance, communication, health services, utilities, construction and wholesale trade.

Demand for manufactured goods, however, fell in September. Factory orders dropped by 1.7% after a 2.9% increase in August, the Commerce Department said. Demand for durable goods was revised downward to a 2.4% decline. The results surprised analysts who had been encouraged by an ISM report earlier this week showing growth in the manufacturing sector.

Initial claims for unemployment benefits fell by 8,000 to 323,000 last week, near the pre-Hurricane Katrina level, the Labor Department said yesterday.

The Economy
Fed Raises Rates, Signals Additional Increases to Come
By Greg Ip
882 words
2 November 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- The Federal Reserve raised short-term interest rates for a 12th consecutive time, and will probably have to keep raising them until the economy slows, to keep inflation from rising.

The Fed, as expected, increased its target for the federal-funds rate, charged on overnight loans between banks, to 4% from 3.75%. Its accompanying statement showed optimism on economic growth and concerns about inflation. "Elevated energy prices and hurricane-related disruptions in economic activity have temporarily depressed output and employment," the Fed said yesterday in the statement accompanying its decision.

In spite of the damage and disruptions caused by hurricanes Katrina and Rita, the U.S. economy grew at a 3.8% annual rate in the third quarter, its eighth consecutive quarter at about that pace. That's above what most economists consider the economy's potential growth rate -- that is, what it can produce with existing capital and labor. Moreover, the Fed appears to see that continuing, thanks to relatively low interest rates, robust productivity growth and "planned rebuilding in the hurricane-affected areas."

At the same time, the Fed warned that, although oil and gasoline prices have fallen from their levels in the immediate aftermath of the storms, the "cumulative rise in energy and other costs have the potential to add to inflation pressures." So far, though, "core" inflation -- which excludes food and energy prices -- has "been relatively low" and "longer-term inflation expectations remain contained."

The Fed suggested it will continue to raise rates, albeit at a "measured" pace, which has come to mean a quarter percentage point per meeting.

All 10 voting members of the policy-setting Federal Open Market Committee agreed on yesterday's increase, unlike Sept. 20 when Fed governor Mark Olson dissented, favoring no increase. (There are normally 12 voting members but two governors' seats are vacant.)

All 12 regional reserve banks agreed to the accompanying increase in the less-important discount rate, charged on short-term Fed loans to commercial banks, to 5% from 4.75%. Many commercial banks raised their prime rate, the benchmark for many business and consumer loans, to 7% from 6.75%.

With the economy running out of spare capacity and showing no signs of slowing, Fed officials have concluded they need to boost rates further than they previously thought to keep higher energy prices from triggering a price-wage spiral. That will likely require the economy to slow down noticeably in the next year or two.

That may happen on its own. There are scattered signs that the housing market is beginning to cool, and high oil prices have eroded consumer confidence.

But without evidence of a slowdown, the Fed is likely to keep raising rates until it induces one. Last month, Fed governor Donald Kohn said that "the economy would still seem poised to expand more quickly than the rate of growth of its sustainable potential unless monetary policy accommodation is removed further."

Markets expect the Fed to raise the funds rate to 4.25% on Dec. 13 and 4.5% on Jan. 31, Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan's last day before retirement. Ben Bernanke, chairman of President Bush's Council of Economic Advisers, has been nominated to succeed Mr. Greenspan. Markets see approximately 50% odds Mr. Bernanke will raise the rate at his first meeting, on March 28, to 4.75%.

Since the Fed began raising the funds rate from a 46-year low of 1% in June last year, it has described rates as "accommodative" -- in other words, below some neutral level that neither stimulates nor restrains growth. Fed watchers had assumed that at some point the Fed would conclude the rate is at neutral and drop "accommodative." Some Fed officials have recently pressed to drop the description.

But it now appears the Fed will keep calling the rate accommodative as long as it expects to raise it further, even if it has passed most estimates of neutral, into "restrictive" territory.

Peter Hooper, chief U.S. economist at Deutsche Bank, thinks the Fed has to get the federal funds rate to 5% because the economy is running out of spare capacity, which heightens the risk that companies and workers will be able to boost prices and wages in response to the jump in energy costs.

Inflation excluding food and energy prices -- the Fed's preferred price index -- was 2% in August and September, near the top of many officials' "comfort range." Mr. Hooper expects inflation to go above that range, forcing the Fed to slow the economy down, to 3% or slower by 2007.

In economic reports yesterday, the Institute for Supply Management said its monthly index was at 59.1% for October, down from September's 59.4% and contrary to expectations for a plunge. With the index continuing to exceed 50 -- seen as an indication that the sector is expanding -- and with new orders and employment continuing to show strength in the report, analysts are optimistic about the sector's future. The Commerce Department said total U.S. construction spending rose 0.5% in September for a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $1.12 trillion.

How to Bargain for a House --- As Market Cools, Buyers Regain Long-Lost Negotiating Power; Want an Audi With That Condo?
By Ruth Simon
1575 words
29 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

HOME BUYERS ARE finally starting to catch a break.

After years of soaring real-estate prices -- not to mention periodic bidding wars for third-rate properties -- inventories of homes for sale are rising in many parts of the country. As a result, some buyers are regaining long-lost bargaining power.

That gives buyers in many markets more room than they have had in years to push sellers to sweeten the pot. Buyers are asking for, and often getting, concessions ranging from help in paying their closing costs to money for repairs or redecorating.

And sellers in many markets -- including once-hot areas like Phoenix, San Diego and Boston -- are finding that they can no longer just slap a price on their home and expect it to move quickly. Increasingly, they are being advised to set more realistic pricing expectations and to spruce up their property with fresh paint or some new plantings out front to stand out from the crowd.

In Rhode Island, where active listings are up 43% from a year earlier, some agents are going even further. Broker Ron Phipps recently offered the buyer of a 2,200-square-foot waterfront condo priced at $550,000 a two-year lease on an Audi TT sports car. The buyer applied the $18,000 value of the lease to the purchase price instead -- then bargained with the seller to kick in an additional $7,000 for closing costs and a new air-conditioning system.

The re-emergence of tactics like these is notable because it could signal the end of a prolonged seller's market in some parts of the country.

Some sellers are even cutting list prices outright. In Phoenix, where the number of homes on the market has climbed to 17,000 from 5,400 three months ago, "we're now constantly getting emails that prices have been reduced," says Charles McLean, broker-owner of Century 21 Metro Alliance. His advice to sellers: go "all-out" with marketing tactics like open houses. As recently as this summer, that wasn't only unnecessary, it was often impossible -- properties sometimes were selling in as little as 24 hours.

In Northern Virginia, Elaine Raabe, the buyer for three stores run by the local Realtors association, says she's having trouble keeping signs that say "price reduced" in stock. For the previous two years, she says, "you couldn't keep an `under contract' sign in the building."

In San Diego, Steve Krescanko of Century 21 Award is advising clients to offer the buyer's broker a 3% commission instead of 2.5%. On a two-bedroom, two-bathroom home in Escondido, Calif., that recently sold for $420,000, the seller combined a 3% payout to the broker with $6,500 toward the buyer's closing costs.

Of course, home sales traditionally slow in the fall. And if mortgage rates remain low, sales could bounce back in the spring. New-home construction surged in September, according to the Commerce Department. Sales of existing homes held steady at their second-highest level on record in September, according to the National Association of Realtors, or NAR. (That's partly because of a boost from Katrina refugees buying homes.)

And many "price cuts" right now are actually deceptive: Sellers who overpriced their properties are being forced to roll back expectations -- but they're still getting more than they probably would have for the same property a year ago.

On Friday, NAR said it expects sales of existing homes to climb 4.8% this year from 2004 to a record 7.1 million. Next year, however, it believes sales will fall 3.5%. NAR expects the median price of an existing home to climb 12% this year, but just 5.3% next year.

Already, there are indications the market is in transition. Rising oil prices and higher interest rates may finally be taking a toll, amid concerns about an overheated housing market. A recent survey of real-estate agents by Banc of America Securities analyst Daniel Oppenheim found that selling times were rising in 19 of 20 markets. Prices in 13 markets were flat or falling, up from four in July.

Although buyers in many markets have the newfound ability to start asking for concessions such as closing costs or repairs, whether they get them is another matter. It depends in part on the dynamics of the local market -- and the willingness of the seller to face reality. "We have sellers who refuse to acknowledge that the boom pricing is over," says David Lereah, chief economist of NAR.

For sellers, it heightens the importance of pricing a home correctly at the outset. Homeowners can no longer look at what their neighbor received and then simply tack on a big premium. Instead, pay attention to recent sales prices of comparable properties and also size up whether the local inventory of homes for sale is rising.

Sellers can also do other things to stand out, such as offering the broker a higher payout if the property is under agreement by a certain date or offering to pay the buyer's closing costs, one month's mortgage payment -- or even the heating bill, says Pat Rioux, broker-owner of ListForLess.com, a flat-fee multiple-listing placement service in Massachusetts.

Not every market is feeling the squeeze. In Charlotte, N.C., sales have been "very brisk," says Pat Riley, president of Allen Tate Co. In Seattle, inventories are down almost 19% from a year ago, says J. Lennox Scott, chairman and chief executive of John L. Scott Real Estate.

But in many areas, some of the excesses of the housing boom are fading. In Washington, D.C., the number of home inspections has climbed because buyers no longer feel they have to forgo inspection to get an offer accepted, says Bo Menkiti, president of the Menkiti Group of Keller Williams Realty.

Also, asking prices are dropping. Crystal Sullivan, an agent with Liz Moore &Associates in Newport News, Va., put a house she owned in Westhaven on the market in late August, priced at $182,900. She sold it six weeks later after offering a $2,500 credit for closing costs and dropping the price to $169,900. "The hurricanes, the gas prices and the war, coupled with the time of year, have really taken its toll on our area," she says.

Alex Fiedorczyk, an investor in the Boston area, put a three-bedroom home on the market in September, priced at $549,000. Since then, he's dropped the price twice, to $499,000, and hired a broker instead of selling it himself. If a buyer doesn't appear before the listing expires in December, Mr. Fiedorczyk plans to pull the house off the market until spring. "It isn't worth another [price] cut," he says.

The average number of showings for homes in Massachusetts fell 25% in September, compared to a year earlier, according to Mapass, an appointment-scheduling service for real-estate brokers.

For some sellers, the readjustment has been painful. David D'Ausilio, an operating partner with Keller Williams Realty in Fairfield County, Conn., says one of his clients had to drop the price on an eight-year-old Colonial in Monroe three times, to $664,900 from $724,900, before accepting an offer this month. Nine to 12 months ago, the home would have sold for as much as $695,000, says Mr. D'Ausilio.

"In certain price points, the market has corrected up to 10% already," he says. For the first time in several years, sellers are accepting offers that are contingent on the buyers selling their own home, he adds.

Linda Baron, an agent with Prudential Fox &Roach, Realtors in the Philadelphia suburb of Blue Bell, Pa., says that hiring a professional "stager" helped her to quickly sell a home priced at $3 million. For about $4,000, the stager brought in silk flowers, throw pillows, bedding and rental furniture and filled a basement storage area with furniture and boxes of collectibles so that the upstairs rooms looked more spacious. "Now that we're seeing houses staying on the market longer, we're going to do it across the board," Ms. Baron says.

The pressure on sellers isn't limited to coastal markets that have seen the biggest gains. In Chicago, where prices have risen steadily but not spectacularly, prices have begun to soften for higher-end properties. "A year ago, you never would hear about going back for a price reduction," says Stephen Baird, president of Baird &Warner. "Now you hear it a little bit." To move a full-floor condo with a view of Lake Michigan, his firm cut the price to $2.975 million from $3.5 million.

In Houston, where inventories are declining, higher energy prices are putting a dent in the sale of existing homes. Prices are down 3% to 5% over the past year or two, says Julius Zatopek III of Re/Max on the Brazos, with homes that were built a decade or more ago taking the biggest hit because they aren't as energy efficient as new construction.

Economy & Politics Brief -- US: New-home-sales rebound is stronger than forecast
By Campion Walsh Dow Jones Newswires

NEW-HOME sales in the U.S. rebounded more quickly than expected in September, while sales for the previous two months were revised down.

The U.S. Commerce Department said Thursday that sales of new single-family homes increased 2.1% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1,222,000. Economy & Politics
U.S. sales of new homes rose 2.1% in September
By Campion Walsh Dow Jones Newswires
514 words
28 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
9
English
(c) 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

WASHINGTON -- U.S. new-home sales rebounded more quickly than expected in September, while sales for the previous two months were revised down.

The U.S. Commerce Department said Thursday that sales of new single-family homes increased 2.1% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.222 million. August sales fell 11.6% to a revised annual rate of 1.197 million from the earlier estimated 1.237 million. Economists surveyed by and CNBC had expected September's sales to rise 1%.

Recent housing-market indicators have yet to show sustained cooling of the red-hot activity of recent years. Existing-home sales were reported unchanged in September at nearly record levels, and housing construction staged a surprising rebound during the month.

The average price of a home fell to $285,700 in September from a revised $287,500 in August. The median price was $215,700 last month, down from the revised $228,800 in August.

The Commerce Department said Thursday that demand for durable goods, or manufactured goods designed to last at least three years, fell 2.1% last month to a seasonally adjusted $207.03 billion. Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires and CNBC had expected a drop of 1%. The decrease marked the second drop in three months and followed a revised 3.8% increase in August, previously reported as a 3.4% gain.

A key barometer of business spending -- orders for nondefense capital goods excluding aircraft -- fell 1.2% after rising 4% the previous month.

Recent reports had presented a mixed picture of the U.S. factory sector. This month the Institute for Supply Management, a private research group, reported its manufacturing index increased 5.8 points in September to 59.4 -- any reading above 50 signals expansion in activity. But the Federal Reserve reported that industrial output fell in September by the sharpest amount in more than 2 1/2 years, dropping 0.5%.

Orders for most goods were down in September, with much of the weakness concentrated in transportation- and computer-related wares. Transportation orders, which have been down three of the last four months, fell 4.7% in September, after increasing 0.8% in August. Commercial-plane orders plummeted 41.6%, and orders for military aircraft dropped 3.5%. But demand for motor vehicles and parts rose 4.7% last month. Excluding transportation, orders for other goods fell 1% in September, after rising 5.1% in August.

Meanwhile, initial jobless claims fell by 28,000 to a seasonally adjusted 328,000 in the week that ended Oct. 22, the U.S. Labor Department said. The four-week average also fell, by 10,000 to 366,500, in the range economists associate with an improving labor market.

---

Antonie Boessenkool and Elizabeth Price contributed to this article

The Economy
Durable-Goods Orders Fall 2.1%, Hurt by Boeing Strike, Katrina
By Joi Preciphs and Kemba Dunham
566 words
28 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

U.S. orders for durable goods fell 2.1% in September, as manufacturers were hit with a strike at Boeing Co. and the aftereffects of Hurricanes Katrina and Rita.

Separate reports yesterday showed that initial unemployment-insurance claims fell to 328,000 from 356,000 last week, and that new-home sales rose last month, but not enough to suppress analysts' view that the housing market is cooling.

The Commerce Department said yesterday that orders for durable goods fell to a seasonally adjusted $207.03 billion. The second drop in three months reverses the 3.8% jump in August.

Manufacturers Alliance/MAPI Chief Economist Daniel J. Meckstroth said, "September was not normal," noting that "a labor strike at Boeing drastically cut aerospace business and . . . Hurricane Katrina battered the Gulf Coast."

The Commerce report shows commercial-aircraft orders plunged 41.6%, while orders for nondefense capital goods, excluding aircraft, fell 1.2%. By that measure, Mr. Meckstroth suggested, the longer-term trend isn't so bad. "Nondefense capital-goods orders excluding aircraft, a good indicator of equipment investment, grew at a 5.3% annual rate in the third quarter, and is up 8.6% above the same period one year ago," he said. "There is no question that manufacturing growth has decelerated, but there still is underlying strength that will lead to a rebound early next year."

Brian Bethune, U.S. economist for Global Insight Inc., in Lexington, Mass., also predicted faster growth lies ahead. "Business investment is still expanding at a solid underlying rate," he said. While growth might be damped slightly for the third quarter, "we are optimistic that a resolution of these issues in the next couple of quarters will set the stage for a near-term re-acceleration," he said.

The Labor Department's report on unemployment benefits showed initial applications fell for the second week in a row. Initial claims fell 28,000 to a seasonally adjusted 328,000, while the four-week moving average fell 10,000 to 366,500 -- in the range analysts say indicates an improving labor market. The department estimates 24,000 of last week's new claims were related to the Gulf hurricanes, pushing the total number of storm-related claims to 502,000.

The Commerce Department reported that new-home sales in September rose 2.1% over August, with single-family home sales rising to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1,222,000, up from August's 1,197,000.

This week several large home builders have reported impressive earnings, including Centex Corp., whose profit rose 59% for the fiscal second quarter ended Sept. 30, and Pulte Homes Inc., whose third-quarter profit climbed 46%. Meritage Homes Corp. nearly doubled its third-quarter profit.

In a conference call, Pulte Chief Executive Richard J. Dugas Jr. said the company saw double-digit growth in new-home orders in all three months of the quarter, though July and August were better than September. Even sales of its active-adult homes in the Washington, D.C., Colorado and Midwest markets -- areas that have been experiencing some weakness -- have been faring well. Still, "the euphoria we've seen in the last few years will probably not be there in '06," he predicted.

Consumer Confidence Fell Again in October
290 words
26 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- High energy costs and the effects of the Gulf Coast hurricanes continue to take their toll, as consumer confidence fell for the second straight month in October. Existing home sales, meanwhile, saw no growth in September, leading some analysts to question continued robust growth in the housing market.

The Conference Board said its consumer-confidence index dropped to 85 from a revised reading of 87.5 in September, following a decline from 105.5 in August. Analysts track consumer sentiment closely as consumer spending accounts for about two-thirds of the U.S. economy. The downward trend, if it continues, could prove worrisome heading into the holiday season when retailers make much of their profit.

Economists originally predicted that the Conference Board index, which is based on survey responses of 5,000 U.S. households, would show an upward trend.

Household reaction to business and labor-market conditions remains skittish. The Conference Board said in its report that its present-situation index fell to 108.2 from September's 110.4, while the expectations index -- which shows consumers' expectations for conditions in the next six months -- slipped to 69.5 from 72. Overall, the number saying that business conditions are good decreased by two points, while those saying that conditions have worsened increased by the same number.

The number of respondents who said jobs are harder to get increased, while those saying jobs are plentiful remained unchanged.

Meanwhile, the National Association of Realtors reported sales of previously owned homes at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 7.28 million units for September, exceeding analysts' expectations for 7.2 million. The number was unchanged from August.

Economy & Politics
Oil, storms sink U.S. confidence
A Wall Street Journal News Roundup
610 words
26 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
9
English
(c) 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

The fallout from devastating storms and high gasoline prices continues to affect the U.S. economy in different ways, as consumer confidence dropped to the weakest level in two years in October and September home sales were flat.

The Conference Board, a private U.S. research group, said its index of consumer confidence for the current month fell to 85 from a revised 87.5 in September and 105.5 in August. Economists had expected to see the index gain some ground and come in at 88. The October reading was the worst since the 81.7 seen in October 2003. The index is derived from responses seen through Oct. 18.

"Much of the decline in confidence over the past two months can be attributed to the recent hurricanes, pump shock [from higher gasoline prices] and a weakening labor market," said Conference Board economist Lynn Franco.

The Conference Board said in its report that its present-situation index fell to 108.2 from September's 110.4, while the expectations index slipped to 69.5 from 72.3.

The drop in expectations suggests that besides higher winter heating bills, consumers may already be thinking about cutting back on Christmas holiday spending, Ms. Franco said. "in order to avoid a blue Christmas, retailers will need to lure shoppers with sales and discounts," she said.

Sales of previously owned homes held steady in September at the second-highest level on record as demand was bolstered by hurricane victims buying homes, the National Association of Realtors said.

Sales of existing homes last month were unchanged from August at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 7.28 million. The Realtors said sales would have fallen without the increased demand for houses because of the devastation from Hurricane Katrina.

The September performance was stronger than economists had been forecasting. They had expected sales to decline by 1.2%, reflecting a belief that rising mortgage rates will begin to cool off the booming housing market. The average interest rate on 30-year fixed-rate mortgages rose to 6.1% last week, its highest level in 15 months, from 6.03% the previous week, according to Freddie Mac, the big government-sponsored mortgage-buying company.

As mortgage rates crept higher, the recent rapid gains in home prices showed hints of moderating. The median price of an existing home stood at $212,000 in September, up by 13.4% from September 2004 -- but that represented a slight slowing from the 15.6% year-over-year price appreciation recorded in August.

Katrina had a major impact on sales in areas where it hit, the Realtors said. Sales plunged by 85% in New Orleans but were up 150% in nearby Baton Rouge, Louisiana. "The nearby regions picked up a great deal of activity very quickly," said David Lereah, chief economist for the Realtors.

Mr. Lereah said the outlook for the housing market is mixed going forward, citing anecdotal reports that there is "some air coming of these very hot markets." Nonetheless, the NAR revised upward its existing-home sales forecast for the year. It sees total existing-home sales of 7.10 million this year, up from 6.78 million in 2004.

The inventory of homes on the market was unchanged at 4.7 months in September, the NAR said. Sales among the regions were mixed. In the Northeast U.S., sales were up 0.8%, while in the Midwest sales fell 3%. Sales in the South rose 3.7% and the West region saw sales decline 4.1%.

The Economy
U.S. Home Construction Jumps; Fed Report Notes Cooling Signs
By Joi Preciphs
563 words
20 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- New U.S. home construction surged last month, confounding expectations for a cooling in the broader housing market after two consecutive months of declines.

Separately, the Federal Reserve had a note of caution on the housing sector with the release of its so-called beige book, a summary of current economic conditions. The Fed noted that while residential real-estate activity "remained generally strong," reports that demand for homes has lessened "have become somewhat more common."

In its report, the Fed said overall economic growth continued in September, with most districts describing "the pace of activity as moderate or gradual." The survey is comprised of information gathered before Oct. 11 from the Fed's 12 regional banks. Along with a drop in oil prices, the beige book was cited as a factor behind yesterday's stock-market rally that lifted the Dow Industrials 128.87 points to 10414.13.

Analysts said rising mortgage rates and the prospect of overbuilding suggest that a downturn may still loom, though reconstruction of the hurricane-ravaged Gulf Coast will sustain the boom in that region.

The Commerce Department reported that housing starts rose 3.4% in September to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 2.108 million units, following a revised 1.2% decline in August. The 3.4% monthly jump was the largest since February and lifted housing starts to a level 10.3% higher than in September 2004.

The gains weren't uniform, however. The South and the Midwest saw construction gains while building in the Northeast and the West remained flat.

"The housing-construction industry continued to work at full capacity," said Brian Bethune, U.S. economist at Global Insight, an international economic and financial research firm based in Boston.

He said building-supply prices, which shot up after the Gulf Coast hurricanes, will eventually affect overall housing construction. "Certainly, it is going to put upward pressure on the cost of housing, which is ultimately going to be passed on to the consumer," Mr. Bethune said. New-home prices as a result will probably rise.

Single-family housing starts rose 2.6% last month to 1.747 million units, the report said. Construction of apartments with two or more units rose 7.8% to 361,000 units; construction of homes with five or more units rose 2.7% to 303,000 units.

Although the latest data available show August new-home sales fell 9.9% to a 1.237 million annual rate, National Association of Realtors analyst Lawrence Yun said home sales "will easily set a new record in 2005." Looking ahead, he cautioned that there has been "a measurable rise in the inventory of unsold new homes that are under construction."

Against the backdrop of mortgage-rate creep, several Fed officials kept up a drumbeat of warnings on inflation, indicating the Fed likely will continue to raise rates.

Unless economic growth "slows unexpectedly . . . the risks may be skewed a little toward the upside on inflation," Fed Governor Donald Kohn said at Carnegie-Mellon University in Pittsburgh. The economy will likely continue to grow faster than its ability to supply goods and services unless interest rates are raised further, he said. "Measured" rate increases, he said, "should forestall" inflation pressures.

Economy & Politics
Housing starts rise 3.4% in U.S. as permits surge
By Jeff Bater Dow Jones Newswires
240 words
20 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
13
English
(c) 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

WASHINGTON -- Housing construction in the U.S. unexpectedly climbed in September, and building permits rose to a 32-year high.

Housing starts increased 3.4% to a seasonally adjusted 2.108 million annual rate, the highest pace in seven months after back-to-back declines, the U.S. Commerce Department said.

Authorizations in September for future building jumped 2.4% to 2.189 million, the highest annual rate since February 1973. Permits had been projected by economists to decline 3.7%.

Wall Street expected a 1.7% decline in September housing starts. The unanticipated increase helped carry construction, year over year, 10.3% higher from September 2004.

"Housing has consistently been a significant driver of the economy and barring any major upset to the economic picture, there's no reason to expect that to change in the near future," said Bob Walters, chief economist at Quicken Loans in Livonia, Michigan.

Analysts expect reconstruction from Hurricane Katrina and the subsequent Hurricane Rita to give a boost to starts somewhere down the road.

Home construction last month climbed in the storm-wracked South, rising 6.9% to 981,000. Starts also increased in the Midwest, up 1.9% to 368,000, while remaining flat at 198,000 in the Northeast and 561,000 in the West.

The Economy
Service-Sector Growth Slows As Costs Rise, Orders Slacken
By Joi Preciphs
587 words
6 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Growth in the service sector slowed considerably in September amid worries about higher costs.

The Institute for Supply Management reported its monthly nonmanufacturing index plunged to a reading of 53.3 last month from 65 in August. The growth rate was the slowest since April 2003, reflecting a deceleration in new orders, employment and new import and export orders, combined with higher prices. The report's price index surged 14 points, to 81.4 in September from 67.1 in August, indicating that service companies saw their costs jumping over the month.

Because a reading of 50 or more is considered a sign of economic solvency, the "overall indication is continued economic growth in the nonmanufacturing sector in September, but at a slower rate of increase than in August," said University of Houston-Downtown Prof. Ralph Kauffman, who conducts the monthly survey of more than 370 purchasing and supply executives.

Six of 17 industries reported declining activity in September, including agriculture, entertainment, real estate, business services, finance and banking, and public administration. Eight reported growth, including construction, insurance, mining, insurance, retail trade, utilities, health services and wholesale trade. Mr. Kauffman said the growth in construction is most likely due to post-Katrina reconstruction efforts. The subindexes for three other categories -- transportation, legal and a broad group of other services -- were flat.

The new-orders index dropped to 56.6 last month, down from 65.8 in August, and employment growth eased to 54.9 from 59.6.

Many industries complained about rising expenses, especially retailers who said "costs are on the rise in almost all sectors due to energy that seems out of control." Others were concerned about the availability and cost of raw materials.

Yesterday's ISM report stands in stark contrast to the organization's cheerier manufacturing report earlier this week. The earlier report indicated the economy would absorb the shock of Hurricane Katrina, and some analysts said they weren't particularly concerned about yesterday's numbers.

"The optics are terrible but we are not too concerned about this," said High Frequency Economics economist Ian Shepherdson in a statement. "We doubt the survey tells us much about the future."

"We're guessing that these businesses have been alarmed by the possible implications of the post-Katrina drop in consumer confidence and the surge in gas prices; both appear to point to a period of softer consumption. But consumer confidence has now bottomed out," Mr. Shepherdson said.

That could change as winter weather -- and the reality of higher heating bills -- sets in. Yesterday, AccuWeather.com predicted a colder-than-average winter, which, combined with higher heating-oil and natural-gas prices, would boost home-heating costs by 50% to 70%.

Although the ISM report showed real-estate activity declining, the National Association of Realtors reported that its index of pending home sales hit another record in August. The index increased 4.7% from a year earlier to a record 129.5. The previous record, 128.1, was set in October 2004. The index measures agreements to purchase homes that haven't been completed.

However, in a sign that the housing market is cooling, the Mortgage Bankers Association said its seasonally adjusted index of applications for mortgages for home purchases slipped 1.9% to 473.8 in the week ended Sept. 30. The index is down about 10% since hitting a peak on June 10.

The Economy
Manufacturing Regains Momentum --- Sector Defies Expectations Of a Decline After Katrina And Jump in Energy Prices
By Joi Preciphs
637 words
4 October 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

American manufacturers, in a pleasant surprise, said business turned up in September despite Hurricane Katrina and the jump in energy prices, a suggestion that the storm didn't do significant damage to the economy outside of the ravaged Gulf Coast.

The Institute for Supply Management reported its monthly index jumped to 59.4% in September from 53.6% in August, a 13-month high. A reading above 50 indicates the manufacturing sector is expanding. Many analysts had expected the September reading to decline.

But manufacturing purchasing managers also reported that prices they paid for materials were up, a sign of incipient inflation.

"The across-the-board improvement in orders, production, employment, and exports confirm that manufacturing outside the Gulf Coast region was not hurt by the hurricane disruptions," Daniel J. Meckstroth, chief economist for the Manufacturers Alliance/MAPI said. "More time is needed, however, to judge whether the September survey fully reflects the impact of production stoppages that occurred in Texas, Louisiana, and Mississippi as part of a national picture."

The National Association of Manufacturers' chief economist, David Huether, called the report "a reassuring bolt from the blue."

The hurricanes, while devastating to those who live in the affected regions, might actually help create jobs, said Nobert Ore, Georgia-Pacific Corp.'s procurement director, who oversees the monthly survey of about 250 purchasing managers. "I suspect as we identify this new demand that comes about from the hurricanes, it might be good for employment," Mr. Ore said. "Obviously the services sector is going to have to compete for labor, as people go down to the region to do construction."

The ISM employment measure inched up to 53.1% in September, from 52.6% in August. Its index of new orders leaped to 63.8% in September from 56.4% in August.

Sixteen industries reported growth last month, including paper and wood-product manufacturers, electronic components and equipment; chemicals; instruments and photographic equipment; transportation; textiles; and rubber and plastic products.

Still, chemical manufacturers reported "some real challenges in getting raw materials," especially those produced in the Gulf region and others shipped through the port of New Orleans. Suppliers also reported steep price increases, due in part to sustained increases in energy costs. The ISM prices-paid index reached 78% in September, up from 62.5% in August.

Although manufacturers have been "very vocal" over the past year about the prices they pay, the lack of comment on the issue in this month's survey possibly reflects manufacturers' realization that circumstances were driving prices, Mr. Ore said. Purchasers were attempting to cover themselves in lieu of supply-chain disruptions, some manufacturers had to move from regular suppliers to spot suppliers to cover their needs, he said. "There's a tendency to pay a little bit more to cover yourself when you have disruptions," he said.

The ISM survey measures the breadth of strength in the manufacturing sector, not the magnitude. The views of a purchasing manager at a small firm count the same as those at a large firm. The report suggests that the hit from Katrina is highly localized, but this Friday's employment report from the Labor Department and subsequent reports on industrial production from the Federal Reserve Board will provide more comprehensive measures.

The Commerce Department also reported yesterday that construction spending in August increased 0.4%. However, Hurricane Katrina came too late in August for its effects to be reflected in the construction-spending report. September's report, released in November, is expected to give a better picture of reconstruction efforts.

International News
Manufacturing In U.S. Expands, But Costs Soar
Associated Press
557 words
4 October 2005
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A2
English
(c) 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

NEW YORK -- The U.S. industrial sector issued an upbeat but also worrisome assessment of itself yesterday: While manufacturing expanded strongly in September, shaking off the impact of Hurricane Katrina, prices for raw materials surged, raising the likelihood of higher inflation and interest rates.

The Institute for Supply Management said its manufacturing index advanced to 59.4 in September from 53.6 the month before, marking the industrial sector's 28th consecutive month of growth. It was the highest reading since the gauge hit 59.6 in August 2004 and well above the 54 reading that analysts had expected.

A reading above 50 indicates the sector is expanding; below 50 indicates manufacturing activity is shrinking. The index is compiled from a survey of purchasing executives in industries across the U.S.

But manufacturers reported a sharp jump in prices last month as higher crude-oil costs and transportation problems caused by the hurricanes boosted input costs. The price index rose to 78 in September from 62.5 in August, the institute said. The price index had jumped 14 points the month before.

Norbert Ore, chair of the institute's business-survey committee, said September's strength came from expansion in new orders and production, but he also noted the possibility of higher inflation.

"While energy prices and the impact from Hurricane Katrina are major concerns, the manufacturing sector has regained significant momentum," Mr. Ore said in a statement accompanying the numbers.

In Washington, meanwhile, the U.S. Commerce Department reported that construction spending climbed to a record in August, helped by a renewed boom in housing. Bigger gains are expected in coming months, spurred by the massive rebuilding required in the aftermath of Katrina, which hit the U.S. Gulf Coast on Aug. 29, and Hurricane Rita, which struck the coast last month.

The Commerce Department said total August construction spending rose by 0.4%, the biggest increase in three months. It pushed building activity to a high of $1.11 trillion at a seasonally adjusted annual rate.

The increase didn't include any hurricane-related activity because Katrina didn't strike until late August. But the government said the spending on the rebuilding of homes and businesses in coming months will add to the construction figures.

Anthony Chan, managing director and senior economist at J.P. Morgan Asset Management in Columbus, Ohio, said the ISM's report reflects "a level of optimism that was not dented as much as one would have expected by Hurricane Katrina."

He said the growth in new orders, production and employment suggested that many manufacturers are operating as if they believe "the effects of Katrina will be short-lived." And, he said, this makes sense "when you start to realize all the stimulus that's going to take place . . . with the replacement of machinery and equipment and the rebuilding efforts in the Gulf states."

The new-orders index expanded to 63.8 in September from 56.4 in August. The production index rose to 63.1 from 55.9 the month before. The employment index rose to 53.1 in September from 52.6 in August.

Letters to the Editor
Realtors: Totally Open And Totally Competitive
278 words
29 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Your Sept. 14 editorial "Justice v. Realtors" concedes that Realtors actually do own their work product -- the listing, which is the agreement between a Realtor and a homeowner that describes the manner in which a home will be marketed and sold. The government should not get involved in dictating to Realtors and homeowners how those marketing plans should be implemented. The industry is already extremely open to competition. It establishes a fair price for its services and then markets its products to the competition, sharing 50% or more of its fee to attract buyers. And it voluntarily makes its inventory available to competitors, empowering them to avoid the costs and infrastructure to produce their own inventory.

When a Realtor presents herself to a prospective home seller and makes a listing presentation, she does so with a full understanding of the pricing pressures and competitive issues. The typical homeowner will invite listing presentations from multiple competitors.

Some Realtors and firms are obviously better than others, and they are able to demand and earn higher fees. Some choose to discount their fees, which may or may not be reflected in their service levels. In the end, the homeowner decides whether the price/value proposition meets his or her needs. Sales associates are almost always independent contractors, and all commission fees are negotiable. A highly efficient marketplace determines what is acceptable. Real estate commissions have been declining for over 10 years, and the national average is 5.1%.

Richard A. Smith

Chairman &CEO

Real Estate Services Division

Cendant Corp.

Parsippany, N.J.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Politics & Economy
U.S. Consumer Sentiment Falls, Housing Market Seems to Cool
By Rafael Gerena-Morales
679 words
28 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

U.S. consumer confidence skidded to a two-year low in September as energy prices soared, and new-home sales fell more than expected in August in a sign the housing market may be cooling.

Taken together -- high energy costs and a potentially cooling housing market -- the reports point to potential troubles for U.S. consumers, who have been squeezed by soaring gasoline prices and who depend increasingly on rising home values to fund their purchases. U.S. consumers have proved resilient to such squeezes in the past, though it remains unclear whether that will be the case this time.

The Conference Board said its consumer-confidence index fell to 86.6 in September. That was down sharply from 105.5 in August and marked the lowest level for the confidence reading since October 2003. The index was equal to 100 in 1985.

The group attributed most of the drop to Hurricane Katrina, which battered the Gulf Coast, causing hundreds of deaths and widespread property damage, while hindering oil production and refining in that region. It also cited soaring gas prices, which were partly the result of Katrina, and a less-optimistic job outlook.

"Historically, shocks have had a short-term impact on consumer confidence, especially on consumers' expectations," said Lynn Franco, director of the Conference Board's Consumer Research Center. "Fuel prices remain high, though they have retreated in recent days, and when combined with a weaker job-market outlook, will likely curb both confidence and spending for the short run. As rebuilding efforts take hold and job growth gains momentum, consumers' confidence should rebound and return to more positive levels by year's end or early 2006."

The Conference Board revised lower its forecast for rising consumer spending to a 1.5% to 2% annual rate for the fourth quarter from an earlier estimate of 3%.

An important wild card for consumers is the state of the housing market. Last year, borrowing against the increasing value of homes added $600 billion, or about 500 billion euros, to household spending power, according to research by Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan. A slowdown in the housing market could sap that source of spending.

The Commerce Department's report on August sales of new single-family homes suggested a slowdown might be at hand, though the evidence is far from conclusive. Sales of single-family homes decreased 9.9% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1,237,000, the Commerce Department said. However, it is a very volatile figure. July sales rose at a 5.3% annual rate to 1,373,000, a figure revised down from an earlier estimate of a record 1.41 million.

Compared with a year earlier, sales still were higher in August. Moreover, the average price of a home rose to $283,400 from a revised $280,000 in July. The median price increased to $220,300 from a revised $215,000 during July.

There were an estimated 479,000 homes for sale at the end of August, representing 4.7 months of supply at the current sales rate. That was the highest inventory level since June 2000, when there was an inventory of 4.8 months of supply. In July, an estimated 467,000 homes were for sale, an inventory of 4.1 months of supply.

One market where inventories of all homes for sale have bloated is Boston. MLS Property Information Network Inc., which provides data on homes for sale in most of Massachusetts, says its total inventory was up 37% at 25,582 homes in mid-September from a year earlier. Some sellers have priced their homes unrealistically high, and buyers "are holding out because they think the bubble is going to burst," said Lisa Coates, a real-estate agent at RE/MAX Advantage in Beverly, Massachusetts.

Politics & Economy
BUSINESS: U.S. Realtors Feast on a Fat Cut -- for Now
By Alan Murray
717 words
28 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

ALAN GREENSPAN THINKS homeowners are powering the U.S. economy. I have a different theory. It's the Realtors.

Think about it. If you or I sell a house, we have to turn around and buy another one -- paying for moving expenses, closing costs, origination fees, etc. No cause for a spending spree there.

But the real-estate brokers walk away with a neat 5% to 6% of the sale. When I sold my inflated house in Washington last month (in order to purchase an inflated house in Fairfield County, Connecticut), the brokers earned enough money to buy a new Lamborghini. In many parts of the world, I could have built a house with what I paid them.

Of course, they worked for their money. On several fine summer Sunday afternoons, they sat around my pool, while my family and I wandered the city, loitering at successive Starbucks. They also devoted great energy and imagination to arguments and schemes devised to persuade us to accept a lower sales price.

For that, they deserve something. But a Lamborghini?

What is remarkable is that in today's economy, this business model survives -- and not just survives, but flourishes, like some hardy breed of insect. Look at what happened to stockbrokers -- a similar breed, who saw their commissions fall from dollars to pennies in the course of three decades. Or take the even more dramatic fate of travel agents, whose commissions on airline travel plummeted from 12% to nothing in just under a decade in the 1990s. In an age when information was scarce, Realtors could claim big commissions, because they controlled the gold -- the information on houses for sale. In an age when information is ubiquitous, it is hard to understand how they continue to rake in such rents. "Technology democratizes information," says Haim Mendelson, a professor at Stanford Graduate School of Business.

FOR THOSE WILLING to use it, the Internet has indeed revolutionized the process of buying a house. In Connecticut, we signed up for a service called MLS-Pulse, provided through a local RE/Max agent. Every time a house came on the market that matched our specifications, I automatically received an email with the details. More than once, I was the first person to see a house after it went on the market.

This was a great service to me, and a breeze for my agent -- who waited for me to call and tell him which houses I wanted to see. Then he called the listing agent, and both showed up to form a phalanx around me as I toured the home -- protecting their respective commissions.

In a saner world, this new use of technology would result in a different way of doing business and significantly lower commissions. So far, however, that hasn't. Steve DelBianco, who as a venture capitalist helped start erealty.com, says that firm started out offering a one-percentage-point rebate to home buyers. But erealty.com has been bought by Prudential Rand Realty and the discounts have largely disappeared.

The relative absence of discounting has prompted the U.S. Justice Department to investigate whether the National Association of Realtors is using illegal tactics to fend off Internet competition. In a suit filed this month, the department charged the association with policies that obstruct brokers who use Internet-based tools, by allowing other brokers to keep their house listings off such services.

"Consumers benefit when real-estate brokers are free to compete vigorously by offering innovative services," says J. Bruce McDonald, the deputy assistant attorney general for antitrust.

THE NATIONAL ASSOCIATION of Realtors disputes the charge, arguing that its policy merely protects the right of brokers and sellers to control whether or not their listings end up on the Internet.

Whether the Justice Department succeeds or not, the world of real-estate brokers will, eventually, change. Realtors won't disappear. But like travel agents, they will shrink in number and have to find new ways to create value for their clients.

Once that happens, attention can turn to the last remaining members of the 6% club: investment bankers.

---
